    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ce90bd6d9f6542e580bbfb264a3819bd">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'deb4edbf-11ff-47ab-9340-0257a8d612dc'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d19177788" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Nitrogen-doped C@MoS<sub>2</sub></b> micropsheres are constructed by a facile one-step hydrothermal method with the aid of polyurethane as carbon and nitrogen sources, and exhibit superior Li and Na ion storage performances (see figure).
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8242dd95-0b83-4af0-8b58-c6e347175fda/chem201601478-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8242dd95-0b83-4af0-8b58-c6e347175fda/chem201601478-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a7625b8b-475a-481c-9519-c26f7869d521/chem201601478-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8242dd95-0b83-4af0-8b58-c6e347175fda/chem201601478-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d19177791" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Rational design and synthesis of advanced anode materials are extremely important for high-performance lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries. Herein, a simple one-step hydrothermal method is developed for fabrication of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres with the help of polyurethane as carbon and nitrogen sources. The MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are composed of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes, which are wrapped by an N-doped carbon layer. Owing to its unique structural features, the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit greatly enhanced lithium- and sodium-storage performances including a high specific capacity, high rate capability, and excellent capacity retention. Additionally, the developed polyurethane-assisted hydrothermal method could be useful for the construction of many other high-capacity metal oxide/sulfide composite electrode materials for energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201601478-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201601478-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Recently, much more attention has focused on fundamental research and technological application of graphene-like transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) because of their unique layered structures and varieties of physical and chemical properties.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0001_R_d19177780e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0004_R_d19177780e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">4</a></span> In TMDs, the molecular layers are formed by the metals and chalcogens which are bonded by strong covalent bonding, and the individual layers are further stacked together by weak van der Waals interactions. Such layered structure, which allows small ions to be reversibly intercalated into the galleries between layers without large volume expansion in its overall dimension, give TMDs significant advantages in the field of reversible energy storage, especially in LIBs and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0005_R_d19177780e379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Science">5</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0009_R_d19177780e379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></span> For example, the optimized WS<sub>2</sub>/reduced graphene oxide composite delivered a capacity of 400–450 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent rate capability performance in lithium storage.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0010_R_d19177780e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a></span> The single-layered ultra-small nanoplates of MoS<sub>2</sub> embedded in carbon nanofibers can maintain 1007 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for lithium storage and 484 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for sodium storage.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0011_R_d19177780e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">11</a></span></p>
         
         <p>As one famous typical graphene-like TMD, the MoS<sub>2</sub> molecular layers are bonded by strong covalent bonding, and the individual layers are further stacked together via weak van der Waals interactions.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0012_R_d19177780e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochem. Commun.">12</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0014_R_d19177780e407" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a></span> Various nanostructured MoS<sub>2</sub> materials are explored as anode materials for lithium/sodium storage.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0013_R_d19177780e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0015_R_d19177780e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0019_R_d19177780e411" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sci. Rep.">19</a></span> Compared with other graphite-based anodes, it exhibits high theoretical capacities (e.g. 670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for lithium storage) and safer operation. Unfortunately, the electrochemical performance of MoS<sub>2</sub> is still far from ideal, and it still suffers from severe capacity fading because of its poor electronic conductivity, structural destruction during cycling, and the generation/shuttle of polysulfides during the charge/discharge process.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0001_R_d19177780e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0014_R_d19177780e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0020_R_d19177780e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0026_R_d19177780e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></span></p>
         
         <p>To overcome these issues, previous work has demonstrated that combining carbon materials (e.g. graphene, carbon nanotubes, and carbon coating) with MoS<sub>2</sub> led to some synergistic effects, by modifying structural stability and triggering enhanced reaction kinetics.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0024_R_d19177780e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0027_R_d19177780e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0032_R_d19177780e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></span> But it is found that there is still much room left to improve their performance. The electrical conductivity in the common pure carbon is not satisfactory, and can cause compromised high-rate performance. Recently, nitrogen doping was identified as an effective way to improve the electrochemical capability by tailoring the electronic and chemical properties of the carbon host in energy storage and conversion devices,<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0033_R_d19177780e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Electrochim. Acta">33</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0035_R_d19177780e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">35</a></span> and meanwhile, improved properties are verified in these systems.</p>
         
         <p>In contrast to previous work, herein, we develop a simple one-step hydrothermal method for the synthesis of nitrogen-doped carbon@MoS<sub>2</sub> (N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub>) microspheres with the aid of polyurethane as the carbon and nitrogen sources. An omnibearing nitrogen-doped carbon coating is realized on the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes forming composite spheres. The preliminary electrochemical lithium- and sodium-storage performances are explored, and enhanced electrochemical performances including high specific capacity and high rate cycling capability are obtained. Our research provides new high-quality carbon/nitrogen sources for advanced anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201601478-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201601478-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0003-title"> Preparation and characterization of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres</h3>
            
            <p>As illustrated in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> a, the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> displays a three-dimensional microsphere architecture with uniform diameters around 200 nm. A high-magnification SEM image shows that the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are made of curved two-dimensional flakes with the thickness of about 10 nm (Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> b). However, the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> only exhibits two-dimensional nanosheets, rather than microspheres, as clearly shown in Figure S1 (see the Supporting Information); the C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite presents an irregular flower-like architecture (Figure S2, Supporting Information), which is composed of nanosheets. Thus the polyurethane has played a vital role for morphology-controlled synthesis of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres. As shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> c and d, the TEM images further present that the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are composed of curved nanoflakes. From the HRTEM image of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres (Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> d), the marked interplanar d-spacing of 0.65 nm corresponds to the (002) lattice plane of the hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> phase. Moreover, the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes are wrapped by a thin amorphous carbon layer, which is further confirmed by the latter Raman and TG analysis. The novel structure was further investigated by the element mapping images of the energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) analysis. As shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> e, the Mo, S, C, and N elements are uniformly distributed over the whole area of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres, indicating a homogenous distribution of N-doped carbon embedded into the MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres. Furthermore, the nitrogen sorption isotherm of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres is shown in Figure S3 (Supporting Information), and the BET surface area is calculated to be 42 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Based on the above results, the microstructure of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are illustrated in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> f and the nitrogen-doped amorphous carbon has successfully embedded into MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres in the hydrothermal process.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1cbc95bc-94f2-4c44-a483-b1dd17e1d473/chem201601478-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1cbc95bc-94f2-4c44-a483-b1dd17e1d473/chem201601478-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d8980d27-694a-4b48-8fd2-335d8280c26a/chem201601478-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1cbc95bc-94f2-4c44-a483-b1dd17e1d473/chem201601478-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Morphological characterization of as-prepared N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres: a–b) SEM images; c–d) TEM and HRTEM (inset); e) element mapping images of Mo, S, C, and N; f) microstructural illustration.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0002">2</a> a shows the XRD pattern of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>, C@MoS<sub>2</sub>, and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres. All the diffraction peaks can be readily indexed to the hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS No.17-1492). But the diffraction peaks of carbon in the C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composites cannot be detected in the XRD patterns because of the low content of amorphous carbon. For theN-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite, it shows conspicuous peaks at 33 and 58.5°, which are indexed as (100) and (110) planes of hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0036_R_d19177780e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0037_R_d19177780e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Small">37</a></span> Moreover, the shift of (002) peaks from 13.6 to 16.0° is triggered by the intercalation of amorphous carbon between the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes as demonstrated in HRTEM (Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0001">1</a> d), which is the same as in previous work.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0038_R_d19177780e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Mater. Chem.">38</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0040_R_d19177780e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem.">40</a></span> In addition, as shown in Figure S4 (Supporting Information), the another diffraction peaks near 8.6° exists for the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite, which is attributed to the spacing of adjacent MoS<sub>2</sub> nanoflakes in amorphous carbon.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0038_R_d19177780e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Mater. Chem.">38</a></span> As clearly seen in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0002">2</a> b, the hexagonal-layered structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets is indicated by two dominant peaks at 378 and 404 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the typical first-order Raman active modes <i>E</i><sub>2g</sub> and <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> due to in-plane vibrational modes within the sulfur–molybdum–sulfur layer.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0022_R_d19177780e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small">22</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0037_R_d19177780e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Small">37</a></span> For the C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composites, two broad peaks located at about 1360 and 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are related to the D and G bands of carbon materials, are observed, besides typical peaks of layered MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. It demonstrates the existence of carbon coating in composites. To determine the weight content of MoS<sub>2</sub> in composites, TG measurements were carried out in air from 20 to 700 °C so that carbon material is oxidized to CO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub> is oxidized to MoO<sub>3</sub>, which is demonstrated by the XRD pattern (JCPDS No.35-0609) (Figure S5, Supporting Information). Based on the final of weight of MoO<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0002">2</a> c), the content of MoS<sub>2</sub> in the C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composites was calculated to approximantely 87.8 and 83.3 wt. %, respectively.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/203b052f-c14f-452a-8ba2-261827d9216c/chem201601478-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/203b052f-c14f-452a-8ba2-261827d9216c/chem201601478-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0f2d2ee5-ba97-4f26-a8d1-ff75b022c31b/chem201601478-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/203b052f-c14f-452a-8ba2-261827d9216c/chem201601478-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres; c) TG curves of the flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres as performed in air.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The chemical state of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres is further analyzed by XPS. As shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0003">3</a> a, the survey scan spectrum shows the existence of Mo, S, C, and N elements in the composite, and, moreover, the calculated atomic ratio of Mo/S is about 1/2. According to the high-resolution Mo 3d spectrum (Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0003">3</a> b), two peaks located at 228.9 and 232.1 eV are observed, which are assigned to Mo<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo<sub>3/2</sub> of the Mo<sup>IV</sup> state, respectively.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0041_R_d19177780e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Energy">41</a></span> Additionally, the peak at 226.3 eV can be indexed as S 2s. Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0003">3</a> c shows the high-resolution of S 2p, which can be deconvoluted into S 3p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 3p<sub>3/2</sub> at 161.9 and 163.1 eV, respectively, indicating that the products are stoichiometric MoS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0003">3</a> d, the N 1s core level can be deconvoluted into two peaks centered at 398.0 and 399.5 eV, which are related to the pyridinic and pyrrolic of N atoms in the carbon coating derived from polyurethane.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0042_R_d19177780e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0043_R_d19177780e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Power Sources">43</a></span> Additionally, according to the XPS analysis, the content of nitrogen in the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres is 1.7 wt. %. Since lithium- or sodium-storage is a kinetic process, the vacancies and electron deficiencies introduced by the nitrogen doping will provide a feasible pathway for lithium or sodium penetration into the host materials, and thus lead to enhanced reversible capacity.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fecc14b6-0d4c-43c2-8b85-d93398e76726/chem201601478-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fecc14b6-0d4c-43c2-8b85-d93398e76726/chem201601478-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bb6871c1-392c-4d64-b85e-39266e1437a8/chem201601478-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fecc14b6-0d4c-43c2-8b85-d93398e76726/chem201601478-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>XPS spectra of theN-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres: a) survey spectrum and high-resolution spectra of Mo 3d (b), S 2p (c), and N 1s (d).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical performance of LIBs</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical reactivity of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for lithium storage was performed by CV measurements. Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0004">4</a> a shows the initial three CV curves at a scanning rate of 0.01 mV s<sup>−1</sup> over the potential range from 3.0 to 0.01 V (vs. Li<sup>+</sup>/Li). In the first cycle, two peaks (one broad peak about 1.1 V and another distinct peak at 0.4 V) are observed, consistent with previous reports.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0017_R_d19177780e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0044_R_d19177780e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Nano">44</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0045_R_d19177780e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></span> The first cathodic peaks centered at 1.1 V are assigned to lithium insertion into the interlayer of MoS<sub>2</sub> to form Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>, accompanied by the phase transformation from the 2H to 1T structure of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0046_R_d19177780e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">46</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0047_R_d19177780e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Energy">47</a></span> The second main peak located at 0.4 V is associated with the further reaction between Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> and Li ion, resulting in Li<sub>2</sub>S and Mo metallic particles.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0024_R_d19177780e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0048_R_d19177780e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Commun.">48</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0049_R_d19177780e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></span> Note that the these two cathodic peaks disappear in the following cycles, indicating irreversibility in the conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> into Li<sub>2</sub>S and Mo. In contrast, a remarkable reduction peak appears at 1.75 V, which is indicative of the formation of Li<sub>2</sub>S.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0048_R_d19177780e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Commun.">48</a></span> In the charge process, two peaks located at around 1.62 and 2.32 V can be observed. The weak broad peak at 1.62 V is assigned to Li<sup>+</sup> associated with Mo reduction, meanwhile the pronounced peak at 2.32 V is indicative of conversion of Li<sub>2</sub>S to sulfur or polysulfide (e.g. S8 and Li<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>).<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0025_R_d19177780e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Asian J.">25</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0034_R_d19177780e754" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a></span> Importantly, they are almost unchanged and remain steady for the CV curves after the first cycle, indicating high reversibility and good stability for lithium storage.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9408ff2e-865a-47f7-942c-ea8981ea4151/chem201601478-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9408ff2e-865a-47f7-942c-ea8981ea4151/chem201601478-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70dec673-60ca-435a-8cd1-4fcd43aaea12/chem201601478-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9408ff2e-865a-47f7-942c-ea8981ea4151/chem201601478-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performances the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for LIBs: a) CV curves from 3.0 to 0.01 V at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) discharge–charge voltage profiles at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; c) cycling stability of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>, flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres at a current density of 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0004">4</a> b shows the discharge–charge profiles of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for the 1st, 2nd, 5th, and 20th cycles at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> between 3.0 and 0.01 V. There are two voltage plateaus at about 1.1 and 0.6 V for the first lithiation process, corresponding to the formation of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> and decomposition of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> into Mo particles embedded in the Li<sub>2</sub>S matrix as well as the formation of a gel-like polymeric layer, respectively.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0036_R_d19177780e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano Energy">36</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0050_R_d19177780e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Nano">50</a></span> During the charge profiles, well-defined plateaus at about 2.3 V are assigned to extraction of lithium from Li<sub>2</sub>S.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0051_R_d19177780e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">51</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0052_R_d19177780e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Angew. Chem.">52</a></span> In the subsequent cycles, two voltage plateaus at 1.9 and 1.2 V can be seen in the discharge curves and two at 2.3 and 1.7 V, corresponding to the two redox reaction of Li/S and Mo/Li, which is in agreement with the above CV results. This electrode exhibits an initial discharge capacity of about 1413 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a coulombic efficiency of about 73 %. The relatively low initial coulombic efficiency is probably caused by some irreversible process such as the formation of the solid-electrolyte interface or decomposition of electrolyte.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0050_R_d19177780e823" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Nano">50</a></span> Nevertheless, the efficiency is maintained at nearly 100 % in the following cycles and the high discharge capacity of about 1050 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is retained after 300 cycles, as shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0004">4</a> c. Interestingly, the capacity gradually increases with cycling (e.g. 970 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2nd cycle increases to 1055 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100th cycle), which is common for various nanostructured anode materials and may be attributed to the growth of the gel-like polymeric layer and possibly electrochemical activation of hybrid composite.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0045_R_d19177780e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0053_R_d19177780e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0054_R_d19177780e834" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Mater.">54</a></span> By contrast, the first discharge capacity and the initial coulombic efficiency of the flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid electrode is about 1416 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 71 %, respectively. But it exhibits gradual capacity decay with cycling and only maintains the discharge capacity of 700 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. Without any carbon modification, the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets show an initial discharge capacity of 1450 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the low initial coulombic efficiency of about 63 % and serious capacity decay. After 100 cycles, the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode only achieves a discharge capacity of about 480 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <p>To evaluate rate capability as an important criterion of the LIB anode, galvanostatical tests were conducted at varying current densities, as shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0005">5</a> a. For the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres, the tenth-cycle capacities of about 1010, 895, 802, 645, and 550 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could be obtained at 150, 300, 600, 1500, and 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, the specific discharge capacity is able to resume about 1020 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 60 cycles with a coulombic efficiency of almost 100 % for each other. For comparison, the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> and flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes show inferior rate performance. For example, the flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode only exhibits a discharge capacity of about 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and recovers a mere discharge capacity of 720 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density reduces to 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. In the case of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>, it delivers terrible rate performance with a slight capacity of about 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. It is thus evident that the high capacity, superior cyclic stability and rate capability results of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are more attractive compared to the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f3e92cfe-14e1-409a-baaa-bc58f4707b00/chem201601478-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f3e92cfe-14e1-409a-baaa-bc58f4707b00/chem201601478-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4ecd98c8-fe1b-46ef-915a-6e8cf3e219d6/chem201601478-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f3e92cfe-14e1-409a-baaa-bc58f4707b00/chem201601478-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>a) Rate capabilities of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>, flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for LIBs and b) the corresponding Nyquist plots at the full charge state (at 3.0 V) after the initial cycle.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The embedded N-doped carbon of MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres are responsible for their excellent electrochemical performance as anode materials of LIBs, which is supported by EIS, as shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0005">5</a> b. It is clearly seen that the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> shows a much smaller radius of semicircle than the pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> and C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, indicating much lower charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) for the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode. The N-doped carbon embedded into the MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres improves the charge transfer at the electrode-electrolyte interface and thus in favor of reversible lithiation–delithiation process.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0024_R_d19177780e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nanoscale">24</a></span> At the low frequency region, a more vertical straight line is observed for the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid electrode. The N-doping of embedded carbon could produce more active defects, which can improve electron conductivity and ion transfer at the interface of the electrode, and thus facilitate lithium-ion diffusion.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0033_R_d19177780e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Electrochim. Acta">33</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0055_R_d19177780e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nanoscale">55</a></span> Meanwhile, the morphologies of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode after 50 cycles are shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0006">6</a>. Although the nanoflakes in the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite are hardly observed after the lithiation–delithiation process, the microsphere morphology is still maintained. This indicates that the embedded N-doped carbon layers could effectively accommodate the volume change and prevent the aggregating. Also, the N-doped carbon layer embedded in the MoS<sub>2</sub> microsphere alleviates the diffusion of soluble polysulfide into the electrolyte during the lithiation–delithiation process.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0026_R_d19177780e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0056_R_d19177780e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">56</a></span> As a result, the electrochemical performance of LIBs, especially the rate capability and long-term capacity retention, of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid electrode is reasonably enhanced.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/15eb8443-c845-4fea-a2b4-9750758a4525/chem201601478-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/15eb8443-c845-4fea-a2b4-9750758a4525/chem201601478-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6645ac27-1993-4e95-9f11-2401cebe9104/chem201601478-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/15eb8443-c845-4fea-a2b4-9750758a4525/chem201601478-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>SEM images (a,b) of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> hybrid electrode for LIBs at a potential of 3.0 V after 50 cycles.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0005-title"> Electrochemical performance of SIBs</h3>
            
            <p>We have explored the sodium-storage performance of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres by comparison of C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite. Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0007">7</a> a shows the CV curves of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for the initial three cycles at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> over the potential range from 3.0 to 0.01 V (Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). In the first discharge sweep, the two peaks are clearly observed at about 1.1 and 0.6 V, respectively, which are associated with sodium-ion insertion between the MoS<sub>2</sub> and embedded N-doped carbon layers, and the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer owing to the decomposition of the electrolyte.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0015_R_d19177780e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0057_R_d19177780e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a>-<a href="#chem201601478-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0059_R_d19177780e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Nano">59</a></span> Below 0.4 V, the sharp peak could be assigned to the electrochemical decomposition of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to form the metallic Mo nanograins embedded into the Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0058_R_d19177780e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">58</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0060_R_d19177780e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ChemSusChem">60</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0061_R_d19177780e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nano Energy">61</a></span> In the charge process, a broad peak was detected at about 1.85 V, indicating the highly reversible oxidation reaction of Na<sub>2</sub>S to polysulfide, which is similar to LIBs.<span><a href="#chem201601478-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0034_R_d19177780e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Power Sources">34</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0051_R_d19177780e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">51</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0059_R_d19177780e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ACS Nano">59</a>, <a href="#chem201601478-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="chem201601478-bib-0061_R_d19177780e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Nano Energy">61</a></span> In addition, the anodic and cathodic peaks from the second cycle are almost overlapped, thus revealing a highly reversible sodiation–desodiation process in the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="chem201601478-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ec7d8e3-cd97-47f0-933b-a41b52d557e0/chem201601478-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ec7d8e3-cd97-47f0-933b-a41b52d557e0/chem201601478-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/067f8ee9-27ba-4718-b22c-d273e3668d89/chem201601478-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ec7d8e3-cd97-47f0-933b-a41b52d557e0/chem201601478-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=chem201601478-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fchem.201601478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performances of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres for SIBs: a) CV curves from 3.0 to 0.01 V at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; b) discharge–charge voltage profiles at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; c) cycling stability at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and d) rate capabilities of flower-like C@MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0007">7</a> b shows the initial three charge–discharge profiles of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode between 3.0 and 0.01 V at a current density of 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Two inclined plateaus in the first discharge process are observed at about 0.6 and 0.2 V, respectively. No conspicuous plateau appears from the second discharge cycling. There is one slop between 1.6 and 2.1 V in all charge profiles, which is consistent with the CV results. The N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode delivers an initial discharge capacity of about 810 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a first coulombic efficiency of 71.5 %. As shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0007">7</a> c, a high capacity retention upon cycling is observed after the initial cycle and it can achieve a stable capacity of about 340 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 150 cycles with a coulombic efficiency of almost 100 %. However, the initial discharge capacity and the corresponding coulombic efficiency of the C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode are 814 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 69.5 %, respectively, and it only achieves 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over 150 cycles. Additionally, we tested the rate capability of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode with comparison of the C@MoS<sub>2</sub>, as shown in Figure <a href="#chem201601478-fig-0007">7</a> d. Impressively, the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibits enhanced high-rate capability with an average capacity of 510 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 300 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 600 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 240 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and 180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the discharge capacity can be recovered to 450 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density is returned to 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at the 60th cycle. By contrast, the C-MoS<sub>2</sub> composite shows low capacity and poor rate capability, only achieving 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high current density of 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and poor capacity recovery (280 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). All these results demonstrate that our designed N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microsphere could be a promising anode material for SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201601478-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201601478-sec-0006-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have explored new high-quality carbon/nitrogen sources (polyurethane) for the construction of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres by a facile one-step hydrothermal method. A uniform N-doped C conductive and protective layer has been realized on MoS<sub>2</sub> flakes to form the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microsphere. In the light of these favorable characteristics, the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres exhibit greatly enhanced lithium- and sodium-storage performances with high capacity and rate capability. Importantly, the polyurethane-assisted hydrothermal methods can be extended to the synthesis of other high-capacity metal oxide/sulfide anode materials for energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="chem201601478-sec-0007">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="chem201601478-sec-0007-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0008-title"> Preparation of N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> microspheres</h3>
            
            <p>Generally, 0.3 g Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub><b>⋅</b>2 H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.5 g CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> were mixed and stirred for 10 min in 70 mL deionized water to form a clear solution. Then, the solution was transferred into a 100 mL Teflon-line stainless steel autoclave with addition of 0.2 g polyurethane, and annealed at 200 °C for 24 h. After cooling naturally, the precipitate was collected by centrifugation with deionized water three times, freeze-dried, and annealed at 600 °C in Ar (heating rate: 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>) for 2 h.</p>
            
            <p>For comparative purposes, pristine MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and a glucose-derived carbon@MoS<sub>2</sub> (C@MoS<sub>2</sub>) composite were fabricated by the similar hydrothermal method without any polyurethane or with 0.2 g glucose.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0009">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0009-title"> Materials characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The morphologies and microstructures of the products were measured by SEM on a Hitachi S4800 microscopy, TEM on JEOL 2100F instrument and XRD on a Rigaku D/Max-2550pc X-ray diffractometer with Cu<sub>Kα</sub> radiation. The nitrogen sorption was measured by using an AUTOSORB-IQ2-MP gas sorption. The Raman spectra were performed by the RenishawinVia Raman microscope using 532 nm laser excitation. The compositions of the N-C@MoS<sub>2</sub> composite were characterized by XPS using an ESCALAB 250Xi X-ray photoelectron spectrometer. The TG measurements of the products were performed by using a Netzsch STA 449C thermal analyzer.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="chem201601478-sec-0010">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="chem201601478-sec-0010-title"> Electrochemical measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The electrodes were prepared by dispersing the as-prepared products (80 wt. %), carbon black (10 wt. %), and poly(vinylidene fluoride) binder (10 wt. %) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent to form a slurry. The resultant slurry was pasted on Ni foil current collectors and dried at 100 °C for 12 h in a vacuum oven. Electrochemical measurements were performed using coin cells (CR2025) with the Li foil as the counter electrode and a polypropylene (PP) microporous film (Cellgard 2300) as the separator (sodium metal as the counter electrode and Whatman glass microfiber as the separator for sodium storage). The electrolyte solution was 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC)-dimethyl carbonate (DMC) with the volume ratio of 1: 1; for the sodium storage, the electrolyte solution was 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a mixture of EC-DMC (1: 1 in volume) with 5 wt. % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive. The CR2025-type coin cells were assembled in a high-purity Ar-filled glove box. The CV curves were performed on a CHI 600D electrochemistry workstation from 3.0 to 0.01 V (vs. Li/Li<sup>+</sup> or Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic discharge-charge measurements were carried out on a LAND battery test system at different current densities between cut-off voltages of 3.0–0.01 V at constant temperature (25±1 °C). The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was obtained from a frequency range of 100 KHz to 0.01 Hz at 5 mV s<sup>−1</sup> at the full charge state (charge to 3.0 V) after the initial cycle. The specific capacities of the as-prepared electrodes were calculated based on the active materials weight.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
